<div id="review_62867881" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/62867881" />
    <a title="Lauryl" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/406662-lauryl"><img alt="Lauryl" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1451712125p2/406662.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/62867881?book_show_action=true">Jul 09, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lauryl" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lauryl" href="/user/show/406662-lauryl">Lauryl</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/406662-lauryl?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/406662-lauryl?shelf=books-i-hated">books-i-hated</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer62867881" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14883403550169301271">Angela Carter, in an essay about Story of the Eye, once said that the French seem to delight so much in shocking the English that they barely take time to notice that the English aren't really all that shocked. Being an American, I can't really know how right she was, but I definitely think that, in a few deft strokes, she captured the guiding spirit of this book.<br/><br/>The problem with "pornography" as literature or art, or even as comedy, is that any criticism one raises to it will be seen by its adv</span>
  <span id="freeText14883403550169301271" style="display:none">Angela Carter, in an essay about Story of the Eye, once said that the French seem to delight so much in shocking the English that they barely take time to notice that the English aren't really all that shocked. Being an American, I can't really know how right she was, but I definitely think that, in a few deft strokes, she captured the guiding spirit of this book.<br /><br />The problem with "pornography" as literature or art, or even as comedy, is that any criticism one raises to it will be seen by its advocates as prudery, whether that is the case or not. <br /><br />Therein lies the major problem with Story of the Eye. I could assure you that I am actually a pretty wanton lass and a great consumer of porn, but it won't matter to people who dug this book. If I didn't dig it (which I didn't), I must be either:<br />a) a fussy, sexless, humorless cold fish<br />or<br />b) too literal-minded and therefore unable to grasp Bataille's deeper philosophical meaning<br /><br />Hey, if Story of the Eye turns you on, then you do your thing. Let your getting-freaky flag fly. <br /><br />As for me, I was not titillated or shocked by it so much as bored and annoyed, and (even as a self-avowed submissive) I've just never found novelistic depictions of violence all that sexy. SOTE was like the literary equivalent of internet trolls who post ridiculous comments using every homophobic, sexist, racist slur they can think of and then, when you object,they accuse you of not being able to take a joke. (Oh, really? I hadn't realized that just using the word "nigger" made a person's post super-hilarious. Where have I been?)<br /><br />Maybe when it was written, the "shock and awe" technique of philoso-literary bludgeonery was still exciting and new, and saying naughty things just to say naughty things still held some freshness as an idea. But in our wonderful postmodern age, if I want to experience something "offensive", I can just turn on Drawn Together or WonderShowzen and let the Hitler jokes come whizzing into my mental space. Awesome! How transgressive of me!<br /><br />The problem with reading SOTE as a novel, besides Bataille's seemingly great love of poo-poo and pee-pee (ha, ha, ha! I say naughty!) is that Bataille is not a novelist. He is a philosopher writing a novel. Compare this for a moment with Nabokov's Lolita, one of the other most famous "pornographic" novels of our time. Nabokov is not a philosopher, but his novel makes us sweat and break because it forces us into the mindset of his pedophiliac protagonist, makes us feel his love and longing for the unwilling object of his illicit desire. It's awful and it's beautiful, it's subjective, and it feels, at the end, like something true has been said. <br /><br />Some people have written in their reviews that SOTE "really isn't about sex" (no sh*t) and have gone on to propose what it IS really about, but I think that the book is a cipher...it means nothing, and is about nothing. It's a collection of words meant to elicit an uncomfortable response without actually defining what it hopes that response will be. It's interesting as a concept, but it makes for a really boring novel.<br /><br />I've never been a big fan of existentialism anyhow, but this book strikes me as existentialism in the most boring of molds: This happened. Then this other thing happened. Then this other thing happened. <br /><br />Yawn.<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14883403550169301271" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_735048969" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/735048969" />
    <a title="Nate D" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/406701-nate-d"><img alt="Nate D" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1203897989p2/406701.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/735048969?book_show_action=true">Oct 05, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nate D" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nate D" href="/user/show/406701-nate-d">Nate D</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">seaside vacation reading</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Nate D by: </span>
      the occultism of memory
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/406701-nate-d?shelf=interwar-maladies">interwar-maladies</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/406701-nate-d?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/406701-nate-d?shelf=france">france</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/406701-nate-d?shelf=read-in-2013">read-in-2013</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer735048969" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1856069860722390413">So I finally read this. I don't really care about porn because porn is boring and utilitarian (and that utility is quite limited at that). Then why was I able to enjoy this? Because though it's pornographic, it's not really porn. Bataille uses that vocabulary to carry out something more ambitious and ambiguous. <br/><br/>The sex acts here rarely involve intercourse, instead enumerating some elaborate convergent alchemy. Blood, milk, semen, eggs, and urine are gradually converted into the eye, the eye, the</span>
  <span id="freeText1856069860722390413" style="display:none">So I finally read this. I don't really care about porn because porn is boring and utilitarian (and that utility is quite limited at that). Then why was I able to enjoy this? Because though it's pornographic, it's not really porn. Bataille uses that vocabulary to carry out something more ambitious and ambiguous. <br /><br />The sex acts here rarely involve intercourse, instead enumerating some elaborate convergent alchemy. Blood, milk, semen, eggs, and urine are gradually converted into the eye, the eye, the eye, the eye, the eye. A specific eye, an obsessive object, staring from and in the face of death.<br /><br />There's a lot here that could obviously be considered pathological, but the narrative itself exists alone and outside all judgement, which is also part of the weird fascination it exerts. Because Bataille manages to this convincing. And while nothing that occurs here is impossible, everything unfolds in such a suspension of the rules of ordinary life and action, that the narrative events ascend into a symbolic order existing well beyond strict reality. And to answer my original question again, I find such refined arcane symbolic orders to be highly enjoyable. This alchemical-symbolic order has such a degree of formal elegance that all the icky sex can barely even be described as icky anymore. It possesses something approaching a purity.<br /><br />There's also a kind of theory here, of significant events and how their signs are branded upon us. The extra materials from later editions and essays in my City Lights copy expand upon that angle excellently. <br /><br />Incidentally, there's a <a href="http://www.premices-the-movie.com/trailer" rel="nofollow noopener">great Swiss film</a> adapted vaguely from this, and the fact that it hits the tone exactly without any actual sex taking place speaks volumes about why both works succeed, I think.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1856069860722390413" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_229302338" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/229302338" />
    <a title="Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2190636-joselito-honestly-and-brilliantly"><img alt="Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1349701607p2/2190636.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/229302338?book_show_action=true">Oct 30, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly" href="/user/show/2190636-joselito-honestly-and-brilliantly">Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer229302338" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17209298883691675066">So far, what have humans done to the written language? You may know genres and sub-genres, but there are written works which can't snugly fit into these categories so you must invent some for them. I suspect that there are these hidden, latent categories in the 1001 books list. Like those which some would place under the category "unreadable, " or "modernist nonsense," or "insomniac pills." So you imagine the editors of the list discussing among themselves which works best exemplify the unreadab</span>
  <span id="freeText17209298883691675066" style="display:none">So far, what have humans done to the written language? You may know genres and sub-genres, but there are written works which can't snugly fit into these categories so you must invent some for them. I suspect that there are these hidden, latent categories in the 1001 books list. Like those which some would place under the category "unreadable, " or "modernist nonsense," or "insomniac pills." So you imagine the editors of the list discussing among themselves which works best exemplify the unreadable ones and one would suggest a James Joyce or a William Gaddis or a Gertrude Stein. Then they'll say, let's put the best ones which can put their readers to sleep, so they put in those novels which get one-star ratings here where the reviewers say they bored them to death. Or the most excellent ones which invite extremely polarized interpretations, reviewers firing off one stars and five stars, disagreeing with each other vehemently. <br /><br />This one made me invent one such a category: hallucinatory porn. The sex here is like this: there are a lot more masturbation than copulation; urine-induced orgasms, death as a sexual stimulus, human eyes gouged out(by a bull's horn; the other eye, almost, by a girl in the height of ecstasy, sucking her partner's eye while having an orgasm), animal testes eaten both cooked and raw, a cunt placed in a bowl of milk (because milk is for pussies) and, through it all, the author reminds you that he's not insane because every now and then he can blurt out some deep philosophies which only geniuses can write and make you somewhat ashamed for being not like them:<br /><br />"To others, the universe seems decent because decent people have gelded eyes. That is why they fear lewdness. They are never frightened by the crowing of a rooster or when strolling under a starry heaven. In general, people savor the 'pleasures of the flesh' only on condition that they be insipid.<br /><br />"But as of then, no doubt existed for me: I did not care for what is known as 'pleasures of the flesh' because they are really insipid; I cared only for what is classified as 'dirty.' On the other hand, I was not even satisfied with the usual debauchery itself, while, in some way or other, anything sublime and perfectly pure is left intact by it. My kind of debauchery soils not only my body and my thoughts, but also anything I may conceive in its course, that is to say, the vast starry universe, which merely serves as a backdrop.<br /><br />"I associate the moon with the vaginal blood of mothers, sisters, that is, the menstrua with their sickening stench..."<br /><br />Later, talking about another book he had written, the author confessed that he had jerked off naked, at night, by his mother's corpse.<br /><br /><br />Simply the best novel there is under this category.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17209298883691675066" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

R and I used to read it to each other in drunken revelry walking down Columbus Avenue, delirious to be alive, ecstatic that someone was going—or rather had gone—much further than we ever thought possible. Kundera writes that a work of art does not necessarily "progress" like sciece does, but inste</span>
  <span id="freeText14076397724111086315" style="display:none">I read those first few pages standing in the doorway of City Lights bookshop when I was nineteen before heading across to Specs for a whiskey. The memory of the milk and the cupboard has never left me. R and I used to read it to each other in drunken revelry walking down Columbus Avenue, delirious to be alive, ecstatic that someone was going—or rather had gone—much further than we ever thought possible. Kundera writes that a work of art does not necessarily "progress" like sciece does, but instead illuminates the unilluminated, the prosaic, the banal. Bataille did just this—his terrible honesty opened a door for me that has never quite shut.</span>

<div id="review_174037287" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/174037287" />
    <a title="Suzanne" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4214678-suzanne"><img alt="Suzanne" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1289255244p2/4214678.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/174037287?book_show_action=true">Jun 05, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Suzanne" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Suzanne" href="/user/show/4214678-suzanne">Suzanne</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer174037287" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14144336085725289569">Ah, Bataille puts Freud to shame in the myriad ways he explores the connections between sex and death in man's mind...Some people have complained that the characters are underdeveloped - if one reads the postscript about Bataille's father, you begin to see that it is intentional. Characters are symbols, symbols are characters, and the whole story is a kaleidoscope of psychological 'isms, and has less to do with sex (if you're looking for good porn, look elsewhere) than to do with having the read</span>
  <span id="freeText14144336085725289569" style="display:none">Ah, Bataille puts Freud to shame in the myriad ways he explores the connections between sex and death in man's mind...Some people have complained that the characters are underdeveloped - if one reads the postscript about Bataille's father, you begin to see that it is intentional. Characters are symbols, symbols are characters, and the whole story is a kaleidoscope of psychological 'isms, and has less to do with sex (if you're looking for good porn, look elsewhere) than to do with having the reader face deep set cultural taboos surrounding sexuality and where they come from. Certainly a fun Rubik's cube for those who love dissecting prose.<br />I'm not sure Simone and Marcelle are meant to be real at all - they are the ID incarnate. There are no warm embraces to be found in these pages, just a dive into the deep end of the pool of the subconscious mind. Or as Jung might say, a walk on the shadow side. Delish.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14144336085725289569" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

The Story of the Eye is a pornographic disintegration of the Western ethical code. It is both magnificent and foul; a more daring and original work than his later philosophy/anthropology. A seminal piece of 20th century literature; although it was published well before the cultural abominations of our current nihilism, we are still not ready for this bleak and punkish work of literar</span>
  <span id="freeText7392969327737565246" style="display:none">George Bataille's brief Sade-esque novella is a mordantly brilliant dip into the post-Nietzschen world modernity. The Story of the Eye is a pornographic disintegration of the Western ethical code. It is both magnificent and foul; a more daring and original work than his later philosophy/anthropology. A seminal piece of 20th century literature; although it was published well before the cultural abominations of our current nihilism, we are still not ready for this bleak and punkish work of literary debauchery.</span>

  <span id="freeText8157919220986090841" style="display:none">I'm a huge fan of Sade's, so when a friend recommended this to me I snatched it from a bookstore the first chance I got. <br /><br />There are some redeeming qualities. The book revolves heavily on fiercely French ideals set by Sade, and though through some mild analysis some genuinely interesting ideas might worm though, I feel the book mainly fails in conveyance. Maybe I'm harsh on this book because I'm comparing it so closely to Sade and his overly explicit way of philosophizing (which most would argue is actually the weaker approach), but I found very little to salvage from this book.<br /><br />Its a book that I'll have to re-read many times in order to have a valid opinion of.</span>

<div id="review_771131436" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/771131436" />
    <a title="Michael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4015835-michael"><img alt="Michael" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1420385695p2/4015835.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/771131436?book_show_action=true">Nov 22, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/4015835-michael">Michael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141185385" href="/book/show/58383.Story_of_the_Eye">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4015835-michael?shelf=1001-books-to-read-before-you-die">1001-books-to-read-before-you-die</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4015835-michael?shelf=translated-fiction">translated-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4015835-michael?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4015835-michael?shelf=erotica">erotica</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4015835-michael?shelf=post-modernism">post-modernism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4015835-michael?shelf=1920s">1920s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4015835-michael?shelf=surreal">surreal</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer771131436" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7031653845995567745">Georges Bataille’s 1928 novella Story of the Eye has often been read for the graphic details of an increasingly inexplicable adventures of a pair of teenagers and their sexual perversions. Narrated by an unnamed male in his late teens, the book tells the story of his passionate affair with Simone, his primary partner. Throughout the book their relationship involves other people including a mentally ill sixteen year-old girl and a voyeuristic English émigré aristocrat. To say this book is risqué </span>
  <span id="freeText7031653845995567745" style="display:none">Georges Bataille’s 1928 novella Story of the Eye has often been read for the graphic details of an increasingly inexplicable adventures of a pair of teenagers and their sexual perversions. Narrated by an unnamed male in his late teens, the book tells the story of his passionate affair with Simone, his primary partner. Throughout the book their relationship involves other people including a mentally ill sixteen year-old girl and a voyeuristic English émigré aristocrat. To say this book is risqué might actually be an understatement, but is the book really about fornication?<br /><br />With a little help from French literary theorist Roland Barthes and his accompanying essay “The Metaphor of the Eye”, I quickly discovered that Story of the Eye is far more complex than I originally thought. However if you do read Georges Bataille’s introduction before the book like I did you will discover a few titbits that help decipher the surrealist nature of the novella. In this introduction Bataille talks about his love/hate relationship with his father, a man who went blind on account of neurosyphilis. He shares a memory he remembers clearly in his head of his father urinating and the vacant look in his milky eyes.<br /><br />The reason this story is important to Story of the Eye is because the novella often references urination and eyes in the midst of the sexual acts. As Barthes explains in his essay, “Although Story of the Eye features a number of named characters with an account of their sex play, Bataille was by no means writing the story of Simone, Marcelle, or the narrator”. The act of sex is often accompanied with some form of violence. The eyes, milk, urine can all be seen as a reference to his memory of his father and any reference to testicles and eggs could be interpreted as metaphors to the creation of life.<br /><br />While Roland Bathes goes into a far deeper analysis of the metaphors found in Story of the Eye, a slight understanding of the content changes this books topic from sexual perversions to an angry rant directed towards Bataille’s father. There are other reference found in the novella that connect to his life; for example the priest. Georges Bataille went into the seminary in the hopes of becoming a priest, however he had to drop out to find a job to support his mother, killing his dream. A topic I believe is discussed in more detail in his non-fiction book Eroticism.<br /><br />I found myself being absorbed in Story of the Eye (which was translated by Joachim Neugroschel, Dovid Bergelson); although difficult to read, the symbolism really intrigued me. So much so that I had to order my own copy of the book in the hopes to re-read it soon. I read this as an ebook and I now own a physical copy which features the essays “The Pornographic Imagination” Susan Sontag and of course “The Metaphor of the Eye”. I am fascinated by the surreal erotic style of Bataille; I think he is an author I need to explore in greater details.<br /><br />This review originally appeared on my blog; <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.knowledgelost.org/literature/book-reviews/genre/erotica/story-of-the-eye-by-georges-bataille/">http://www.knowledgelost.org/literatu...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="7031653845995567745" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      if (form) {

It is a long narrative prose poem which celebrates the principle of Evil in a<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText7754726175301177570\" style=\"display:none\">The macabre but beautiful work, Les Chants de Maldoror, has achieved a considerable reputation as one of the earliest and most extraordinary examples of Surrealist writing. It is a long narrative prose poem which celebrates the principle of Evil in an elaborate style and with a passion akin to religious fanaticism. The French poet-critic Georges Hugnet has written of Lautréamont: &quot;He terrifies, stupefies, strikes dumb. He could look squarely at that which others had merely given a passing glance.&quot;\n\n\nLittle is known of the author of Maldoror, Isidore Ducasse, self-styled Comte de Lautréamont, except that he was born in Montevideo, Uruguay in 1846 and died in Paris at the age of twenty-four. When first published in 1868-9, Maldoror went almost unnoticed. But in the nineties the book was rediscovered and hailed as a work of genius by such eminent writers as Huysmans, Léon Bloy, Maeterlinck, and Rémy de Gourmont. Later still, Lautréamont was to be canonized as one of their principal &quot;ancestors&quot; by the Paris Surrealists.\n\n\nThis edition, translated by Guy Wernham, includes also a long introduction to a never-written, or now lost, volume of poetry. Thus, except for a few letters, it gives all the surviving literary work of Lautréamont.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"7754726175301177570\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return The Thirst for Annihilation is the first book in English to respond to Bataille\'s writings. In no way, though, is Nick Land\'s book an attempt to appropriate Bataille\'s writings to a secular intelligibility or to compromise with the aridity of academic discourse - rather, it is written as a communion. Theoretical issues in philosophy, sociology, psychodynamics, politics and poetry are discussed, but only as stepping stones into the deep water of textual sacrifice where words pass over into the broken voice of death. Cultural modernity is diagnosed down to its Kantian bedrock with its transcendental philosophy of the object, but Bataille\'s writings cut violently across this tightly disciplined reading to reveal A carefully woven ass<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText4004136077123466185\" style=\"display:none\">When Marcel Schwob published &quot;The Book of Monelle&quot; in French in 1894, it immediately became the unofficial bible of the French Symbolist movement, admired by such contemporaries as Stéphane Mallarmé, Alfred Jarry and André Gide. A carefully woven assemblage of legends, aphorisms, fairy tales and nihilistic philosophy, it remains a deeply enigmatic and haunting work more than a century later, a gathering of literary and personal ruins written in a style that evokes both the Brothers Grimm and Friedrich Nietzsche. &quot;The Book of Monelle&quot; was the result of Schwob\'s intense emotional suffering over the loss of his love, a &quot;girl of the streets&quot; named Louise, whom he had befriended in 1891 and who succumbed to tuberculosis two years later. Transforming her into the innocent prophet of destruction, Monelle, Schwob tells the stories of her various sisters: girls succumbing to disillusionment, caught between the misleading world of childlike fantasy and the bitter world of reality. This new translation reintroduces a true fin-de-siècle masterpiece into English.A secret influence on generations of writers, from Guillaume Apollinaire and Jorge Luis Borges to Roberto Bolaño, Marcel Schwob (1867-1905) was as versed in the street slang of medieval thieves as he was in the poetry of Walt Whitman (whom he translated into French). Deleuze\'s essay, certainly the most profound study <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText10958121941848710432\" style=\"display:none\">In his stunning essay, Coldness and Cruelty, Gilles Deleuze provides a rigorous and informed philosophical examination of the work of the late 19th-century German novelist Leopold von Sacher-Masoch. Deleuze\'s essay, certainly the most profound study yet produced on the relations between sadism and masochism, seeks to develop and explain Masoch\'s &quot;peculiar way of \'desexualizing\' love while at the same time sexualizing the entire history of humanity.&quot; He shows that masochism is something far more subtle and complex than the enjoyment of pain, that masochism has nothing to do with sadism; their worlds do not communicate, just as the genius of those who created them - Masoch and Sade - lie stylistically, philosophically, and politically poles a part. Venus in Furs, the most famous of all of Masoch\'s novels was written in 1870 and belongs to an unfinished cycle of works that Masoch entitled The Heritage of Cain. The cycle was to treat a series of themes including love, war, and death. The present work is about love. Although the entire constellation of symbols that has come to characterize the masochistic syndrome can be found here - fetishes, whips, disguises, fur-clad women, contracts, humiliations, punishment, and always the volatile presence of a terrible coldness - these do not eclipse the singular power of Masoch\'s eroticism.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"10958121941848710432\" href=\"#\"       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/20842.Georges_Bataille">About Georges Bataille</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/20842.Georges_Bataille"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1601203548p3/20842.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/20842.Georges_Bataille">Georges Bataille
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
1,917
followers
</div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer17428382925229860418">French essayist, philosophical theorist, and novelist, often called the "metaphysician of evil." Bataille was interested in sex, death, degradation, and the power and potential of the obscene. He rejected traditional literature and considered that the ultimate aim of all intellectual, artistic, or religious activity should be the annihilation of the rational individual in a violent, transcendental</span>
  <span id="freeText17428382925229860418" style="display:none">French essayist, philosophical theorist, and novelist, often called the "metaphysician of evil." Bataille was interested in sex, death, degradation, and the power and potential of the obscene. He rejected traditional literature and considered that the ultimate aim of all intellectual, artistic, or religious activity should be the annihilation of the rational individual in a violent, transcendental act of communion. Roland Barthes, Julia Kristeva, and Philippe Sollers have all written enthusiastically about his work. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17428382925229860418" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>

En tocava un per cap, però les dues escriptores que tenien prevista la seva participació van declinar l’oferiment i dos d’ells van haver de repetir per arrodonir les deu pometes d’aquest pomer literari. El jurat els va concedir el premi, i en cap moment va sospitar que el pseudònim encobrís vuit autors que, a més, ja eren coneguts en l’àmbit de la cultura catalana. La sorpresa va ser grossa. Es va publicar l’any 1980 i també es va traduir al castellà. Els autors havien acordat recuperar per a la literatura catalana les maneres desimboltes i la riquesa del llenguatge popular referit a la sexualitat, però no s’esperaven que els personatges i les situacions dels diversos contes acabessin coincidint, descomptada la disparitat de les històries, en la lleugeresa i la gràcia candent de cada pàgina.       <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/616919-histoire-de-l-il">Quotes from Story of the Eye</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;To others, the universe seems decent because decent people have gelded eyes. That is why they fear lewdness. They are never frightened by the crowing of a rooster or when strolling under a starry heaven. In general, people savor the "pleasures of the flesh" only on condition that they be insipid.<br />But as of then, no doubt existed for me: I did not care for what is known as "pleasures of the flesh" because they really are insipid; I cared only for what is classified as "dirty." On the other hand, I was not even satisfied with the usual debauchery, because the only thing it dirties is debauchery itself, while, in some way or other, anything sublime and perfectly pure is left intact by it. My kind of debauchery soils not only my body and my thoughts, but also anything I may conceive in its course, that is to say, the vast starry universe, which merely serves as a backdrop.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
